links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<p></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mythology">Mythology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Typhon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Mythology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Birth">Birth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Typhon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Birth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Hesiod" title="Hesiod">Hesiod</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Theogony" title="Theogony">Theogony</a></i> (c. 8th \xe2\x80\x93 7th century BC), Typhon was the son of <a href="/wiki/Gaia_(mythology)" title="Gaia (mythology)">Gaia</a> (Earth) and <a href="/wiki/Tartarus" title="Tartarus">Tartarus</a>: "when <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a> had driven the <a href="/wiki/Titan_(mythology)" title="Titan (mythology)">Titans</a> from heaven, huge Earth bore her youngest child Typhoeus of the love of Tartarus, by the aid of golden <a href="/wiki/Aphrodite" title="Aphrodite">Aphrodite</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> The mythographer <a href="/wiki/Bibliotheca_(Pseudo-Apollodorus)" title="Bibliotheca (Pseudo-Apollodorus)">Apollodorus</a> (1st or 2nd century AD) adds that Gaia bore Typhon in anger at the gods for their destruction of her offspring the <a href="/wiki/Giants_(Greek_mythology)" title="Giants (Greek mythology)">Giants</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Numerous other sources mention Typhon as being the offspring of Gaia, or simply "earth-born", with no mention of Tartarus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> However, according to the <a href="/wiki/Homeric_Hymns" title="Homeric Hymns"><i>Homeric Hymn to Apollo</i></a> (6th century BC), Typhon was the child of <a href="/wiki/Hera" title="Hera">Hera</a> alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Hera, angry at Zeus for having given birth to <a href="/wiki/Athena" title="Athena">Athena</a> by himself, prayed to Gaia, <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a>, and the Titans, to give her a son stronger than Zeus, then slapped the ground and became pregnant. Hera gave the infant Typhon to the serpent <a href="/wiki/Python_(mythology)" title="Python (mythology)">Python</a> to raise, and Typhon grew up to become a great bane to mortals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wenceslas_Hollar_-_The_Greek_gods._Tryphon.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1648" data-file-width="2256" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Wenceslas_Hollar_-_The_Greek_gods._Tryphon.jpg/300px-Wenceslas_Hollar_-_The_Greek_gods._Tryphon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Wenceslas_Hollar_-_The_Greek_gods._Tryphon.jpg/450px-Wenceslas_Hollar_-_The_Greek_gods._Tryphon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Wenceslas_Hollar_-_The_Greek_gods._Tryphon.jpg/600px-Wenceslas_Hollar_-_The_Greek_gods._Tryphon.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wenceslas_Hollar_-_The_Greek_gods._Tryphon.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nDepiction by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wenceslas_Hollar" title="Wenceslas Hollar">Wenceslas Hollar</a></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Several sources locate Typhon\'s birth and dwelling place in <a href="/wiki/Cilicia" title="Cilicia">Cilicia</a>, and in particular the region in the vicinity of the ancient Cilician coastal city of <a href="/wiki/Corycus" title="Corycus">Corycus</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/K%C4%B1zkalesi" title="K\xc4\xb1zkalesi">K\xc4\xb1zkalesi</a>, Turkey). The poet <a href="/wiki/Pindar" title="Pindar">Pindar</a> (c. 470 BC) calls Typhon "Cilician,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> and says that Typhon was born in Cilicia and nurtured in "the famous Cilician cave",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> an apparent allusion to the <a href="/wiki/Corycus#Corycian_Cave" title="Corycus">Corycian cave</a> in Turkey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Aeschylus" title="Aeschylus">Aeschylus</a>\' <i><a href="/wiki/Prometheus_Bound" title="Prometheus Bound">Prometheus Bound</a></i>, Typhon is called the "dweller of the Cilician caves",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> and both Apollodorus and the poet <a href="/wiki/Nonnus" title="Nonnus">Nonnus</a> (4th or 5th century AD) have Typhon born in Cilicia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The b scholia to <i><a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a></i> 2.783, preserving a possibly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orphic" title="Orphic">Orphic</a> tradition, has Typhon born in Cilicia, as the offspring of <a href="/wiki/Cronus" title="Cronus">Cronus</a>. Gaia, angry at the destruction of the Giants, slanders Zeus to Hera. So Hera goes to Zeus\' father Cronus (whom Zeus had overthrown) and Cronus gives Hera two eggs smeared with his own semen, telling her to bury them, and that from them would be born one who would overthrow Zeus. Hera, angry at Zeus, buries the eggs in Cilicia "under Arimon", but when Typhon is born, Hera, now reconciled with Zeus, informs him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Descriptions">Descriptions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Typhon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Descriptions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Hesiod" title="Hesiod">Hesiod</a>, Typhon was "terrible, outrageous and lawless",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> immensely powerful, and on his shoulders were one hundred snake heads, that emitted fire and every kind of noise:</p>\n<blockquote>\n<p>Strength was with his hands in all that he did and the feet of the strong god were untiring. From his shoulders grew a hundred heads of a snake, a fearful dragon, with dark, flickering tongues, and from under the brows of his eyes in his marvellous heads flashed fire, and fire burned from his heads as he glared. And there were voices in all his dreadful heads which uttered every kind of sound unspeakable; for at one time they made sounds such that the gods understood, but at another, the noise of a bull bellowing aloud in proud ungovernable fury; and at another, the sound of a lion, relentless of heart; and at another, sounds like whelps, wonderful to hear; and again, at another, he would hiss, so that the high mountains re-echoed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Homeric_Hymns" title="Homeric Hymns"><i>Homeric Hymn to Apollo</i></a> describes Typhon as "fell" and "cruel", and neither like gods nor men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Three of <a href="/wiki/Pindar" title="Pindar">Pindar</a>\'s poems have Typhon as hundred-headed (as in Hesiod),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> while apparently a fourth gives him only fifty heads,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> but a hundred heads for Typhon became standard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Chalcis" title="Chalcis">Chalcidian</a> <a href="/wiki/Hydria" title="Hydria">hydria</a> (c. 540\xe2\x80\x93530 BC), depicts Typhon as a winged humanoid from the waist up, with two snake tails below.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Aeschylus" title="Aeschylus">Aeschylus</a> calls Typhon "fire-breathing".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> For <a href="/wiki/Nicander" title="Nicander">Nicander</a> (2nd century BC), Typhon was a monster of enormous strength, and strange appearance, with many heads, hands, and wings, and with huge snake coils coming from his thighs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Apollodorus describes Typhon as a huge winged monster, whose head "brushed the stars", human in form above the waist, with snake coils below, and fire flashing from his eyes:</p>\n<blockquote>\n<p>In size and strength he surpassed all the offspring of Earth. As far as the thighs he was of human shape and of such prodigious bulk that he out-topped all the mountains, and his head often brushed the stars. One of his hands reached out to the west and the other to the east, and from them projected a hundred dragons\' heads. From the thighs downward he had huge coils of vipers, which when drawn out, reached to his very head and emitted a loud hissing. His body was all winged: unkempt hair streamed on the wind from his head and cheeks; and fire flashed from his eyes.</p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>The most elaborate description of Typhon is found in <a href="/wiki/Nonnus" title="Nonnus">Nonnus</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Dionysiaca" title="Dionysiaca">Dionysiaca</a></i>. Nonnus makes numerous references to Typhon\'s serpentine nature,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> giving him a "tangled army of snakes",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> snaky feet,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> and hair.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> According to Nonnus, Typhon was a "poison-spitting viper",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> whose "every hair belched viper-poison",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> and Typhon "spat out showers of poison from his throat; the mountain torrents were swollen, as the monster showered fountains from the viperish bristles of his high head",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> and "the water-snakes of the monster\'s viperish feet crawl into the caverns underground, spitting poison!".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Following Hesiod and others, Nonnus gives Typhon many heads (though untotaled), but in addition to snake heads,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Nonnus also gives Typhon many other animal heads, including leopards, lions, bulls, boars, bears, cattle, wolves, and dogs, which combine to make \'the cries of all wild beasts together\',<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> and a "babel of screaming sounds".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Nonnus also gives Typhon "legions of arms innumerable",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> and where Nicander had only said that Typhon had "many" hands, and Ovid had given Typhon a hundred hands, Nonnus gives Typhon two hundred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Offspring">Offspring</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Typhon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Offspring">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Hesiod" title="Hesiod">Hesiod</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Theogony" title="Theogony">Theogony</a></i>, Typhon "was joined in love" to <a href="/wiki/Echidna_(mythology)" title="Echidna (mythology)">Echidna</a>, a monstrous half-woman and half-snake, who bore Typhon "fierce offspring".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> First, according to Hesiod, there was <a href="/wiki/Orthrus" title="Orthrus">Orthrus</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> the two-headed dog who guarded the Cattle of <a href="/wiki/Geryon" title="Geryon">Geryon</a>, second <a href="/wiki/Cerberus" title="Cerberus">Cerberus</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> the multiheaded dog who guarded the gates of <a href="/wiki/Hades" title="Hades">Hades</a>, and third the <a href="/wiki/Lernaean_Hydra" title="Lernaean Hydra">Lernaean Hydra</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> the many-headed serpent who, when one of its heads was cut off, grew two more. The <i>Theogony</i> next mentions an ambiguous "she", which might refer to Echidna, as the mother of the <a href="/wiki/Chimera_(mythology)" title="Chimera (mythology)">Chimera</a> (a fire-breathing beast that was part lion, part goat, and had a snake-headed tail) with Typhon then being the father.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>\n<p>While mentioning Cerberus and "other monsters" as being the offspring of Echidna and Typhon, the mythographer <a href="/wiki/Acusilaus" title="Acusilaus">Acusilaus</a> (6th century BC) adds the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caucasian_Eagle" title="Caucasian Eagle">Caucasian Eagle</a> that ate the liver of <a href="/wiki/Prometheus" title="Prometheus">Prometheus</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> the mythographer <a href="/wiki/Pherecydes_of_Leros" title="Pherecydes of Leros">Pherecydes of Leros</a> (5th century BC), also names Prometheus\' eagle,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> and adds <a href="/wiki/Ladon_(mythology)" title="Ladon (mythology)">Ladon</a> (though Pherecydes does not use this name), and the dragon that guarded the <a href="/wiki/Golden_apple" title="Golden apple">golden apples</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Garden_of_the_Hesperides" title="Garden of the Hesperides">Garden of the Hesperides</a> (according to Hesiod, the offspring of Ceto and Phorcys).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> The lyric poet Lasus of Hermione (6th century BC) adds the Sphinx.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Later authors mostly retain these offspring of Typhon by Echidna, while adding others. <a href="/wiki/Bibliotheca_(Pseudo-Apollodorus)" title="Bibliotheca (Pseudo-Apollodorus)">Apollodorus</a>, in addition to naming as their offspring Orthrus, the Chimera (citing Hesiod as his source) the Caucasian Eagle, Ladon, and the Sphinx, also adds the Nemean lion (no mother is given), and the <a href="/wiki/Crommyonian_Sow" title="Crommyonian Sow">Crommyonian Sow</a>, killed by the hero <a href="/wiki/Theseus" title="Theseus">Theseus</a> (unmentioned by Hesiod).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>\n<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyginus" title="Hyginus">Hyginus</a> (1st century BC),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> in his list of offspring of Typhon (all by Echidna), retains from the above: Cerberus, the Chimera, the Sphinx, the Hydra and Ladon, and adds "Gorgon" (by which Hyginus means the mother of <a href="/wiki/Medusa" title="Medusa">Medusa</a>, whereas Hesiod\'s three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gorgons" title="Gorgons">Gorgons</a>, of which Medusa was one, were the daughters of Ceto and Phorcys), the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colchian_Dragon" title="Colchian Dragon">Colchian Dragon</a> that guarded the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Fleece" title="Golden Fleece">Golden Fleece</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Scylla" title="Scylla">Scylla</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harpies" title="Harpies">Harpies</a>, in Hesiod the daughters of <a href="/wiki/Thaumas" title="Thaumas">Thaumas</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Oceanid" title="Oceanid">Oceanid</a> <a href="/wiki/Oceanid" title="Oceanid">Electra</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> in one source, are said to be the daughters of Typhon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The sea serpents which attacked the Trojan priest <a href="/wiki/Laoco%C3%B6n" title="Laoco\xc3\xb6n">Laoco\xc3\xb6n</a>, during the <a href="/wiki/Trojan_War" title="Trojan War">Trojan War</a>, were perhaps supposed to be the progeny of Typhon and Echidna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>\n<p>According to Hesiod, the defeated Typhon is the source of destructive storm winds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Battle_with_Zeus">Battle with Zeus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Typhon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Battle with Zeus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Typhon challenged <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a> for rule of the cosmos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> The earliest mention of Typhon, and his only occurrence in <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a>, is a passing reference in the <i><a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a></i> to Zeus striking the ground around where Typhon lies defeated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hesiod" title="Hesiod">Hesiod</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Theogony" title="Theogony">Theogony</a></i> gives us the first account of their battle. According to Hesiod, without the quick action of Zeus, Typhon would have "come to reign over mortals and immortals".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> In the <i>Theogony</i> Zeus and Typhon meet in cataclysmic conflict:</p>\n<blockquote>\n<p>[Zeus] thundered hard and mightily: and the earth around resounded terribly and the wide heaven above, and the sea and Ocean\'s streams and the nether parts of the earth. Great Olympus reeled beneath the divine feet of the king as he arose and earth groaned thereat. And through the two of them heat took hold on the dark-blue sea, through the thunder and lightning, and through the fire from the monster, and the scorching winds and blazing thunderbolt. The whole earth seethed, and sky and sea: and the long waves raged along the beaches round and about at the rush of the deathless gods: and there arose an endless shaking. Hades trembled where he rules over the dead below, and the Titans under Tartarus who live with Cronos, because of the unending clamor and the fearful strife.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>Zeus with his thunderbolt easily overcomes Typhon,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> who is thrown down to earth in a fiery crash:</p>\n<blockquote>\n<p>So when Zeus had raised up his might and seized his arms, thunder and lightning and lurid thunderbolt, he leaped from Olympus and struck him, and burned all the marvellous heads of the monster about him. But when Zeus had conquered him and lashed him with strokes, Typhoeus was hurled down, a maimed wreck, so that the huge earth groaned. And flame shot forth from the thunderstricken lord in the dim rugged glens of the mount, when he was smitten. A great part of huge earth was scorched by the terrible vapor and melted as tin melts when heated by men\'s art in channelled crucibles; or as iron, which is hardest of all things, is shortened by glowing fire in mountain glens and melts in the divine earth through the strength of Hephaestus. Even so, then, the earth melted in the glow of the blazing fire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>Defeated, Typhon is cast into <a href="/wiki/Tartarus" title="Tartarus">Tartarus</a> by an angry Zeus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Epimenides" title="Epimenides">Epimenides</a> (7th or 6th century BC) seemingly knew a different version of the story, in which Typhon enters Zeus\' palace while Zeus is asleep, but Zeus awakes and kills Typhon with a thunderbolt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pindar" title="Pindar">Pindar</a> apparently knew of a tradition which had the gods, in order to escape from Typhon, transform themselves into animals, and flee to Egypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pindar" title="Pindar">Pindar</a> calls Typhon the "enemy of the gods",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> and says that he was defeated by Zeus\' thunderbolt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> In one poem Pindar has Typhon being held prisoner by Zeus under Etna,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> and in another says that Typhon "lies in dread Tartarus", stretched out underground between Mount Etna and <a href="/wiki/Cumae" title="Cumae">Cumae</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> In Aeschylus\' <i>Prometheus Bound</i>, a "hissing" Typhon, his eyes flashing, "withstood all the gods", but "the unsleeping bolt of Zeus" struck him, and "he was burnt to ashes and his strength blasted from him by the lightning bolt."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>\n<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Pherecydes_of_Leros" title="Pherecydes of Leros">Pherecydes of Leros</a>, during his battle with Zeus, Typhon first flees to the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, which begins to burn, then to the volcanic island of Pithecussae (modern <a href="/wiki/Ischia" title="Ischia">Ischia</a>), off the coast of Cumae, where he is buried under the island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Apollonius_of_Rhodes" title="Apollonius of Rhodes">Apollonius of Rhodes</a> (3rd century BC), like Pherecydes, presents a multi-stage battle, with Typhon being struck by Zeus\' thunderbolt on mount <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, before fleeing to the mountains and plain of Nysa, and ending up (as already mentioned by the fifth-century BC Greek historian <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a>) buried under <a href="/wiki/Lake_Bardawil" title="Lake Bardawil">Lake Serbonis</a> in Egypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Like Pindar, <a href="/wiki/Nicander" title="Nicander">Nicander</a> has all the gods but Zeus and <a href="/wiki/Athena" title="Athena">Athena</a>, transform into animal forms and flee to Egypt: <a href="/wiki/Apollo" title="Apollo">Apollo</a> became a hawk, <a href="/wiki/Hermes" title="Hermes">Hermes</a> an ibis, <a href="/wiki/Ares" title="Ares">Ares</a> a fish, <a href="/wiki/Artemis" title="Artemis">Artemis</a> a cat, <a href="/wiki/Dionysus" title="Dionysus">Dionysus</a> a goat, <a href="/wiki/Heracles" title="Heracles">Heracles</a> a fawn, <a href="/wiki/Hephaestus" title="Hephaestus">Hephaestus</a> an ox, and <a href="/wiki/Leto" title="Leto">Leto</a> a mouse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The geographer <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a> (c. 20 AD) gives several locations which were associated with the battle. According to Strabo, Typhon was said to have cut the serpentine channel of the <a href="/wiki/Orontes_River" title="Orontes River">Orontes River</a>, which flowed beneath the <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syrian</a> Mount Kasios (modern <a href="/wiki/Jebel_Aqra" title="Jebel Aqra">Jebel Aqra</a>), while fleeing from Zeus,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> and some placed the battle at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catacecaumene" title="Catacecaumene">Catacecaumene</a> ("Burnt Land"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> a volcanic plain, on the upper <a href="/wiki/Gediz_River" title="Gediz River">Gediz River</a>, between the ancient kingdoms of <a href="/wiki/Lydia" title="Lydia">Lydia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mysia" title="Mysia">Mysia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phrygia" title="Phrygia">Phrygia</a>, near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mount_Tmolus" title="Mount Tmolus">Mount Tmolus</a> (modern Bozda\xc4\x9f) and <a href="/wiki/Sardis" title="Sardis">Sardis</a> the ancient capital of Lydia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In the versions of the battle given by Hesiod, Aeschylus and Pindar, Zeus\' defeat of Typhon is straightforward, however a more involved version of the battle is given by Apollodorus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> No early source gives any reason for the conflict, but Apollodorus\' account seemingly implies that Typhon had been produced by Gaia to avenge the destruction, by Zeus and the other gods, of the Giants, a previous generation of offspring of Gaia. According to Apollodorus, Typhon, "hurling kindled rocks", attacked the gods, "with hissings and shouts, spouting a great jet of fire from his mouth." Seeing this, the gods transformed into animals and fled to Egypt (as in Pindar and Nicander). However "Zeus pelted Typhon at a distance with thunderbolts, and at close quarters struck him down with an adamantine sickle"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Wounded, Typhon fled to the Syrian Mount Kasios, where Zeus "grappled" with him. But Typhon, twining his snaky coils around Zeus, was able to wrest away the sickle and cut the sinews from Zeus\' hands and feet. Typhon carried the disabled Zeus across the sea to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corycian_cave_(Turkey)" title="Corycian cave (Turkey)">Corycian cave</a> in Cilicia where he set the she-serpent <a href="/wiki/Delphyne" title="Delphyne">Delphyne</a> to guard over Zeus and his severed sinews, which Typhon had hidden in a bearskin. But <a href="/wiki/Hermes" title="Hermes">Hermes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aegipan" title="Aegipan">Aegipan</a> (possibly another name for <a href="/wiki/Pan_(god)" title="Pan (god)">Pan</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> stole the sinews and gave them back to Zeus. His strength restored, Zeus chased Typhon to mount Nysa, where the <a href="/wiki/Moirai" title="Moirai">Moirai</a> tricked Typhon into eating "ephemeral fruits" which weakened him. Typhon then fled to <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">Thrace</a>, where he threw mountains at Zeus, which were turned back on him by Zeus\' thunderbolts, and the mountain where Typhon stood, being drenched with Typhon\'s blood, became known as Mount Haemus (Bloody Mountain). Typhon then fled to <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>, where Zeus threw <a href="/wiki/Mount_Etna" title="Mount Etna">Mount Etna</a> on top of Typhon burying him, and so finally defeated him.</p>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Oppian" title="Oppian">Oppian</a> (2nd century AD) says that Pan helped Zeus in the battle by tricking Typhon to come out from his lair, and into the open, by the "promise of a banquet of fish", thus enabling Zeus to defeat Typhon with his thunderbolts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>\n<h4><span id="Nonnus\'s_Dionysiaca"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Nonnus.27s_Dionysiaca">Nonnus\'s <i>Dionysiaca</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Typhon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Nonnus\'s Dionysiaca">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The longest and most involved version of the battle appears in <a href="/wiki/Nonnus" title="Nonnus">Nonnus</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Dionysiaca" title="Dionysiaca">Dionysiaca</a></i> (late 4th or early 5th century AD).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Zeus hides his thunderbolts in a cave, so that he might seduce the maiden <a href="/wiki/Plouto_(mother_of_Tantalus)" title="Plouto (mother of Tantalus)">Plouto</a>, and so produce <a href="/wiki/Tantalus" title="Tantalus">Tantalus</a>. But smoke rising from the thunderbolts, enables Typhon, under the guidance of Gaia, to locate Zeus\'s weapons, steal them, and hide them in another cave.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Immediately Typhon extends "his clambering hands into the upper air" and begins a long and concerted attack upon the heavens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Then "leaving the air" he turns his attack upon the seas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Finally Typhon attempts to wield Zeus\' thunderbolts, but they "felt the hands of a novice, and all their manly blaze was unmanned."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Now Zeus\' sinews had somehow \xe2\x80\x93 Nonnus does not say how or when \xe2\x80\x94 fallen to the ground during their battle, and Typhon had taken them also.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> But Zeus devises a plan with <a href="/wiki/Cadmus" title="Cadmus">Cadmus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pan_(god)" title="Pan (god)">Pan</a> to beguile Typhon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Cadmus, desguised as a shepherd, enchants Typhon by playing the panpipes, and Typhon entrusting the thuderbolts to Gaia, sets out to find the source of the music he hears.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Finding Cadmus, he challenges him to a contest, offering Cadmus any goddess as wife, excepting Hera whom Typhon has reserved for himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Cadmus then tells Typhon that, if he liked the "little tune" of his pipes, then he would love the music of his lyre \xe2\x80\x93 if only it could be strung with Zeus\' sinews.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> So Typhon retrieves the sinews and gives them to Cadmus, who hides them in another cave, and again begins to play his bewitching pipes, so that "Typhoeus yielded his whole soul to Cadmos for the melody to charm".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>\n<p>With Typhon distracted, Zeus takes back his thunderbolts. Cadmus stops playing, and Typhon, released from his spell, rushes back to his cave to discover the thunderbolts gone. Incensed Typhon unleashes devastation upon the world: animals are devoured, (Typhon\'s many animal heads each eat animals of its own kind), rivers turned to dust, seas made dry land, and the land "laid waste".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The day ends with Typhon yet unchallenged, and while the other gods "moved about the cloudless Nile", Zeus waits through the night for the coming dawn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Victory "reproaches" Zeus, urging him to "stand up as champion of your own children!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Dawn comes and Typhon roars out a challenge to Zeus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> And a cataclysmic battle for "the sceptre and throne of Zeus" is joined. Typhon piles up mountains as battlements and with his "legions of arms innumerable", showers volley after volley of trees and rocks at Zeus, but all are destroyed, or blown aside, or dodged, or thrown back at Typhon. Typhon throws torrents of water at Zeus\' thunderbolts to quench them, but Zeus is able to cut off some of Typhon\'s hands with "frozen volleys of air as by a knife", and hurling thunderbolts is able to burn more of typhon\'s "endless hands", and cut off some of his "countless heads". Typhon is attacked by the four winds, and "frozen volleys of jagged hailstones."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Gaia tries to aid her burnt and frozen son.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Finally Typhon falls, and Zeus shouts out a long stream of mocking taunts, telling Typhon that he is to be buried under Sicily\'s hills, with a <a href="/wiki/Cenotaph" title="Cenotaph">cenotaph</a> over him which will read "This is the barrow of Typhoeus, son of Earth, who once lashed the sky with stones, and the fire of heaven burnt him up".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Burial_and_cause_of_volcanic_activity">Burial and cause of volcanic activity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Typhon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Burial and cause of volcanic activity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Etna_and_Ischia">Etna and Ischia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Typhon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Etna and Ischia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Most accounts have the defeated Typhon buried under either <a href="/wiki/Mount_Etna" title="Mount Etna">Mount Etna</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>, or the volcanic island of <a href="/wiki/Ischia" title="Ischia">Ischia</a>, the largest of the <a href="/wiki/Phlegraean_Islands" title="Phlegraean Islands">Phlegraean Islands</a> off the coast of <a href="/wiki/Naples" title="Naples">Naples</a>, with Typhon being the cause of volcanic eruptions and earthquakes.</p>\n<p>Though Hesiod has Typhon simply cast into <a href="/wiki/Tartarus" title="Tartarus">Tartarus</a> by Zeus, some have read a reference to Mount Etna in Hesiod\'s description of Typhon\'s fall:</p>\n<blockquote>\n<p>And flame shot forth from the thunderstricken lord in the dim rugged glens of the mount when he was smitten. A great part of huge earth was scorched by the terrible vapor and melted as tin melts when heated by men\'s art in channelled crucibles; or as iron, which is hardest of all things, is shortened by glowing fire in mountain glens and melts in the divine earth through the strength of Hephaestus. Even so, then, the earth melted in the glow of the blazing fire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>The first certain references to Typhon buried under Etna, as well as being the cause of its eruptions, occur in Pindar:</p>\n<blockquote>\n<p>Son of Cronus, you who hold Aetna, the wind-swept weight on terrible hundred-headed Typhon, <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>and:</p>\n<blockquote>\n<p>among them is he who lies in dread Tartarus, that enemy of the gods, Typhon with his hundred heads. Once the famous Cilician cave nurtured him, but now the sea-girt cliffs above Cumae, and Sicily too, lie heavy on his shaggy chest. And the pillar of the sky holds him down, snow-covered Aetna, year-round nurse of bitter frost, from whose inmost caves belch forth the purest streams of unapproachable fire. In the daytime her rivers roll out a fiery flood of smoke, while in the darkness of night the crimson flame hurls rocks down to the deep plain of the sea with a crashing roar. That monster shoots up the most terrible jets of fire; it is a marvellous wonder to see, and a marvel even to hear about when men are present. Such a creature is bound beneath the dark and leafy heights of Aetna and beneath the plain, and his bed scratches and goads the whole length of his back stretched out against it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>Thus Pindar has Typhon in Tartarus, and buried under not just Etna, but under a vast volcanic region stretching from Sicily to <a href="/wiki/Cumae" title="Cumae">Cumae</a> (in the vicinity of modern <a href="/wiki/Naples" title="Naples">Naples</a>), a region which presumably also included <a href="/wiki/Mount_Vesuvius" title="Mount Vesuvius">Mount Vesuvius</a>, as well as Ischia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Many subsequent accounts mention either Etna<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> or Ischia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> In <i><a href="/wiki/Prometheus_Bound" title="Prometheus Bound">Prometheus Bound</a></i>, Typhon is imprisoned underneath Etna, while above him <a href="/wiki/Hephaestus" title="Hephaestus">Hephaestus</a> "hammers the molten ore", and in his rage, the "charred" Typhon causes "rivers of fire" to pour forth. <a href="/wiki/Ovid" title="Ovid">Ovid</a> has Typhon buried under all of Sicily, with his left and right hands under <a href="/wiki/Punta_del_Faro" title="Punta del Faro">Pelorus</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pachynus" title="Pachynus">Pachynus</a>, his feet under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lilybaeum" title="Lilybaeum">Lilybaeus</a>, and his head under Etna; where he "vomits flames from his ferocious mouth". And <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Valerius_Flaccus" title="Gaius Valerius Flaccus">Valerius Flaccus</a> has Typhon\'s head under Etna, and all of Sicily shaken when Typhon "struggles". <a href="/wiki/Lycophron" title="Lycophron">Lycophron</a> has both Typhon and <a href="/wiki/Giants_(Greek_mythology)" title="Giants (Greek mythology)">Giants</a> buried under the island of Ischia. <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silius_Italicus" title="Silius Italicus">Silius Italicus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Claudian" title="Claudian">Claudian</a>, all calling the island "Inarime", have Typhon buried there. Strabo, calling Ischia "Pithecussae", reports the "myth" that Typhon lay buried there, and that when he "turns his body the flames and the waters, and sometimes even small islands containing boiling water, spout forth."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In addition to Typhon, other mythological beings were also said to be buried under Mount Etna and the cause of its volcanic activity. Most notably the Giant <a href="/wiki/Enceladus_(mythology)" title="Enceladus (mythology)">Enceladus</a> was said to be entombed under Etna, the volcano\'s eruptions being the breath of Enceladus, and its tremors caused by the Giant rolling over from side to side beneath the mountain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Also said to be buried under Etna were the Hundred-hander <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Briareus" title="Briareus">Briareus</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> and Asteropus who was perhaps one of the <a href="/wiki/Cyclops" title="Cyclops">Cyclopes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Boeotia">Boeotia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Typhon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Boeotia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Typhon\'s final resting place was apparently also said to be in <a href="/wiki/Boeotia" title="Boeotia">Boeotia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hesiodic" title="Hesiodic">Hesiodic</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Shield_of_Heracles" title="Shield of Heracles">Shield of Heracles</a></i> names a mountain near <a href="/wiki/Thebes,_Greece" title="Thebes, Greece">Thebes</a> Typhaonium, perhaps reflecting an early tradition which also had Typhon buried under a <a href="/wiki/Boeotia" title="Boeotia">Boeotian</a> mountain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> And some apparently claimed that Typhon was buried beneath a mountain in Boeotia, from which came exhaltations of fire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span id=\'"Couch_of_Typhoeus"\'></span><span class="mw-headline" id=".22Couch_of_Typhoeus.22">"Couch of Typhoeus"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Typhon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title=\'Edit section: "Couch of Typhoeus"\'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Homer describes a place he calls the "couch [or bed] of Typhoeus", which he locates in the land of the Arimoi (<i>\xce\xb5\xe1\xbc\xb0\xce\xbd \xe1\xbc\x88\xcf\x81\xce\xaf\xce\xbc\xce\xbf\xce\xb9\xcf\x82</i>), where Zeus lashes the land about Typhoeus with his thunderbolts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> Presumably this is the same land where, according to Hesiod, Typhon\'s mate Echidna keeps guard "in Arima" (<i>\xce\xb5\xe1\xbc\xb0\xce\xbd \xe1\xbc\x88\xcf\x81\xce\xaf\xce\xbc\xce\xbf\xce\xb9\xcf\x83\xce\xb9\xce\xbd</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></p>\n<p>But neither Homer nor Hesiod say anything more about where these Arimoi or this Arima might be. The question of whether an historical place was meant, and its possible location, has been, since ancient times, the subject of speculation and debate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a> discusses the question in some detail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> Several locales, <a href="/wiki/Cilicia" title="Cilicia">Cilicia</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Syria" title="History of Syria">Syria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lydia" title="Lydia">Lydia</a>, and the island of <a href="/wiki/Ischia" title="Ischia">Ischia</a>, all places associated with Typhon, are given by Strabo as possible locations for Homer\'s "Arimoi".</p>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Pindar" title="Pindar">Pindar</a> has his Cilician Typhon slain by Zeus "among the Arimoi",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> and the historian <a href="/wiki/Callisthenes" title="Callisthenes">Callisthenes</a> (4th century BC), located the Arimoi and the Arima mountains in Cilicia, near the <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6ksu" title="G\xc3\xb6ksu">Calycadnus</a> river, the Corycian cave and the Sarpedon promomtory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> The b scholia to <i><a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a></i> 2.783, mentioned above, says Typhon was born in Cilicia "under Arimon",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Nonnus" title="Nonnus">Nonnus</a> mentions Typhon\'s "bloodstained cave of Arima" in Cilicia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Just across the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Issus" title="Gulf of Issus">Gulf of Issus</a> from <a href="/wiki/Corycus" title="Corycus">Corycus</a>, in ancient Syria, was Mount Kasios (modern <a href="/wiki/Jebel_Aqra" title="Jebel Aqra">Jebel Aqra</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/Orontes_River" title="Orontes River">Orontes River</a>, sites associated with Typhon\'s battle with Zeus,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> and according to Strabo, the historian <a href="/wiki/Posidonius" title="Posidonius">Posidonius</a> (c. 2nd century BC) identified the Arimoi with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aramaeans" title="Aramaeans">Aramaeans</a> of Syria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Alternatively, according to Strabo, some placed the Arimoi at Catacecaumene,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Xanthus_(historian)" title="Xanthus (historian)">Xanthus of Lydia</a> (5th century BC) added that "a certain Arimus" ruled there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> Strabo also tells us that for "some" Homer\'s "couch of Typhon" was located "in a wooded place, in the fertile land of Hyde", with Hyde being another name for <a href="/wiki/Sardis" title="Sardis">Sardis</a> (or its acropolis), and that <a href="/wiki/Demetrius_of_Scepsis" title="Demetrius of Scepsis">Demetrius of Scepsis</a> (2nd century BC) thought that the Arimoi were most plausibly located "in the Catacecaumene country in Mysia".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> The 3rd-century BC poet <a href="/wiki/Lycophron" title="Lycophron">Lycophron</a> placed the lair of Typhons\' mate Echidna in this region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Another place, mentioned by Strabo, as being associated with Arima, is the island of <a href="/wiki/Ischia" title="Ischia">Ischia</a>, where according to <a href="/wiki/Pherecydes_of_Leros" title="Pherecydes of Leros">Pherecydes of Leros</a>, Typhon had fled, and in the area where Pindar and others had said Typhon was buried. The connection to Arima, comes from the island\'s Greek name Pithecussae, which derives from the Greek word for monkey, and according to Strabo, residents of the island said that "arimoi" was also the Etruscan word for monkeys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Typhon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Name">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Typhon\'s name has a number of variants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> The earliest forms of Typhoeus and Typhaon, occur prior to the 5th century BC. Homer uses Typhoeus,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> Hesiod and the <i>Homeric Hymn to Apollo</i> use both Typhoeus and Typhaon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> The later forms Typhos and Typhon occur from the 5th century BC onwards, with Typhon becoming the standard form by the end of that century.</p>\n<p>Though several possible derivations of the name Typhon have been suggested, the derivation remains uncertain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> Consistent with Hesiod\'s making storm winds Typhon\'s offspring, some have supposed that Typhon was originally a wind-god, and ancient sources associated him with the Greek words <i>tuphon, tuphos</i> meaning "whirlwind".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> Other theories include derivation from a Greek root meaning "smoke" (consistent with Typhon\'s identification with volcanoes),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> from an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-European_root" title="Indo-European root">Indo-European root</a> meaning "abyss" (making Typhon a "Serpent of the Deep"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> and from Sap\xc3\xb5n the <a href="/wiki/Phoenician_language" title="Phoenician language">Phoenician</a> name for the <a href="/wiki/Ugaritic" title="Ugaritic">Ugaritic</a> god <a href="/wiki/Baal" title="Baal">Baal</a>\'s holy mountain <a href="/wiki/Jebel_Aqra" title="Jebel Aqra">Jebel Aqra</a> (the classical Mount Kasios) associated with the epithet <a href="/wiki/Baal-zephon" title="Baal-zephon">Ba\xca\xbfal Sap\xc5\x8dn</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparative_mythology">Comparative mythology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Typhon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Comparative mythology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Succession_myth">Succession myth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Typhon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Succession myth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Typhonomachy, Zeus\' battle and defeat of Typhon, is just one part of a larger "Succession Myth" given in Hesiod\'s <i>Theogony</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> The Hesiodic succession myth describes how <a href="/wiki/Uranus_(mythology)" title="Uranus (mythology)">Uranus</a>, the original ruler of the cosmos, hid his offspring away inside <a href="/wiki/Gaia_(mythology)" title="Gaia (mythology)">Gaia</a>, but was overthrown by his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Titans_(mythology)" title="Titans (mythology)">Titan</a> son <a href="/wiki/Cronus" title="Cronus">Cronus</a>, who castrated Uranus, and how in turn, Cronus, who swallowed his children as they were born, was himself overthrown by his son Zeus, whose mother had given Cronus a stone wrapped in swaddling clothes to swallow, in place of Zeus. However Zeus is then confronted with one final adversary, Typhon, which he quickly defeats. Now clearly the supreme power in the cosmos, Zeus is elected king of gods. Zeus then establishes and secures his realm through the apportionment of various functions and responsibilities to the other gods, and by means of marriage. Finally, by swallowing his first wife <a href="/wiki/Metis_(mythology)" title="Metis (mythology)">Metis</a>, who was destined to produce a son stronger than himself, Zeus is able to put an end to the cycle of succession.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Python">Python</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Typhon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Python">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Typhon\'s story seems related to that of another monstrous offspring of Gaia: <a href="/wiki/Python_(mythology)" title="Python (mythology)">Python</a>, the serpent killed by <a href="/wiki/Apollo" title="Apollo">Apollo</a> at <a href="/wiki/Delphi" title="Delphi">Delphi</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> suggesting a possible common origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> Besides the similarity of names, their shared parentage, and the fact that both were snaky monsters killed in single combat with an Olympian god, there are other connections between the stories surrounding Typhon, and those surrounding <a href="/wiki/Python_(mythology)" title="Python (mythology)">Python</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Although the Delphic monster killed by <a href="/wiki/Apollo" title="Apollo">Apollo</a> is usually said to be the male serpent Python, in the <i>Homeric Hymn to Apollo</i>, the earliest account of this story, the god kills a nameless she-serpent (<i><a href="/wiki/Drakaina_(mythology)" title="Drakaina (mythology)">drakaina</a></i>), subsequently called <a href="/wiki/Delphyne" title="Delphyne">Delphyne</a>, who had been Typhon\'s foster-mother.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> Delphyne and Echidna, besides both being intimately connected to Typhon\xe2\x80\x94one as mother, the other as mate\xe2\x80\x94share other similarities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> Both were half-maid and half-snake,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> a plague to men,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> and associated with the Corycian cave in Cilicia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Python was also perhaps connected with a different <a href="/wiki/Corycian_Cave" title="Corycian Cave">Corycian Cave</a> than the one in Cilicia, this one on the slopes of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parnassus" title="Parnassus">Parnassus</a> above Delphi, and just as the Corcian cave in Cilicia was thought to be Typhon and Echidna\'s lair, and associated with Typhon\'s battle with Zeus, there is evidence to suggest that the Corycian cave above Delphi was supposed to be Python\'s (or Delphyne\'s) lair, and associated with his (or her) battle with Apollo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Near_Eastern_influence">Near Eastern influence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Typhon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Near Eastern influence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>From at least as early as Pindar, and possibly as early as Homer and Hesiod (with their references to the Arimoi and Arima), Typhon\'s birth place and battle with Zeus were associated with various Near East locales in Cilicia and Syria, including the Corycian Cave, Mount Kasios, and the Orontes River. Besides this coincidence of place, the Hesiodic succession myth, (including the Typhonomachy), as well as other Greek accounts of these myths, exhibit other parallels with several <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Near_East" title="Ancient Near East">ancient Near Eastern</a> antecedents, and it is generally held that the Greek accounts are intimately connected with, and influenced by, these Near Eastern counterparts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> In particular, the Typhonomachy is generally thought to have been influenced by several Near Eastern monster-slaying myths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup></p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Typhon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Mesopotamia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Three related god vs. monster combat myths from <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, date from at least the early second-millennium BC or earlier. These are the battles of the god Ninurta with the monsters Asag and Anzu, and the god Marduk\'s battle with the monstrous Tiamat.</p>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Ninurta_vs._Asag">Ninurta vs. Asag</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Typhon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Ninurta vs. Asag">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<p><i><a href="/wiki/Lugal-e" title="Lugal-e">Lugal-e</a></i>, a late-third-millennium BC <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerian</a> poem, tells the story of the battle between the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesopotamian_religion" title="Mesopotamian religion">Mesopotamian</a> hero-god <a href="/wiki/Ninurta" title="Ninurta">Ninurta</a> and the terrible monster <a href="/wiki/Asag" title="Asag">Asag</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> Like Typhon, Asag was a monstrous hissing offspring of Earth (<a href="/wiki/Ki_(goddess)" title="Ki (goddess)">Ki</a>), who grew mighty and challenged the rule of Ninurta, who like Zeus, was a storm-god employing winds and floods as weapons. As in Hesiod\'s account of the Typhonomachy, during their battle, both Asag and Ninurta set fire to the landscape. And like Apollodorus\' Typhon, Asag evidently won an initial victory, before being finally overcome by Ninurta.</p>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Ninurta_vs._Anzu">Ninurta vs. Anzu</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Typhon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Ninurta vs. Anzu">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chaos_Monster_and_Sun_God.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1484" data-file-width="2012" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Chaos_Monster_and_Sun_God.png/220px-Chaos_Monster_and_Sun_God.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Chaos_Monster_and_Sun_God.png/330px-Chaos_Monster_and_Sun_God.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Chaos_Monster_and_Sun_God.png/440px-Chaos_Monster_and_Sun_God.png 2x" width="220"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chaos_Monster_and_Sun_God.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<a href="/wiki/Ninurta" title="Ninurta">Ninurta</a> with his thunderbolts battles the winged <a href="/wiki/Anz%C3%BB_(mythology)" title="Anz\xc3\xbb (mythology)">Anzu</a>, palace relief, <a href="/wiki/Nineveh" title="Nineveh">Nineveh</a>.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The early second millennium BC <a href="/wiki/Akkadian" title="Akkadian">Akkadian</a> epic <i>Anzu</i> tells the story of another combat of Ninurta with a monstrous challenger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> His foe is the winged monster <a href="/wiki/Anz%C3%BB_(mythology)" title="Anz\xc3\xbb (mythology)">Anzu</a>, another offspring of Earth. Like Hesiod\'s Typhon, Anzu roared like a lion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> and was the source of destructive storm winds. Ninurta destroys Anzu on a mountainside, and is portrayed as lashing the ground where Anzu lay with a rainstorm and floodwaters, just as Homer has Zeus lash the land about Typhon with his thundebolts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup></p>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Marduk_vs._Tiamat">Marduk vs. Tiamat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Typhon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Marduk vs. Tiamat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<p>The early second-millenium BC <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonian</a>-<a href="/wiki/Akkadian" title="Akkadian">Akkadian</a> creation epic <i><a href="/wiki/En%C3%BBma_Eli%C5%A1" title="En\xc3\xbbma Eli\xc5\xa1">En\xc3\xbbma Eli\xc5\xa1</a></i> tells the story of the battle of the Babylonian supreme god <a href="/wiki/Marduk" title="Marduk">Marduk</a> with <a href="/wiki/Tiamat" title="Tiamat">Tiamat</a>, the Sea personified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> Like Zeus, Marduk was a storm-god, who employed wind and lightning as weapons, and who, before he can succeed to the kingship of the gods, must defeat a huge and fearsome enemy in single combat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> This time the monster is female, and may be related to the Pythian dragoness <a href="/wiki/Delphyne" title="Delphyne">Delphyne</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> or Typhon\'s mate Echidna, since like Echidna, Tiamat was the mother of a brood of monsters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup></p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mount_Kasios">Mount Kasios</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Typhon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Mount Kasios">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Like the Typhonomachy, several Near East myths, tell of battles between a storm-god and a snaky monster associated with Mount Kasios, the modern <a href="/wiki/Jebel_Aqra" title="Jebel Aqra">Jebel Aqra</a>. These myths are usually considered to be the origins of the myth of Zeus\xe2\x80\x99s battle with Typhon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup></p>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Baal_Sapon_vs._Yam">Baal Sapon vs. Yam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Typhon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Baal Sapon vs. Yam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<p>From the south side of the Jebel Aqra, comes the tale of <a href="/wiki/Baal-zephon" title="Baal-zephon">Baal Sapon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Yam_(god)" title="Yam (god)">Yam</a>, the deified Sea (like Tiamat above). Fragmentary <a href="/wiki/Ugaritic" title="Ugaritic">Ugaritic</a> tablets, dated to the fourteenth or thirteenth-century BC, tell the story of the <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">Canaanite</a> storm-god Baal Sapon\xe2\x80\x99s battle against the monstrous Yam on Mount Sapuna the Canaanite name for later Greeks\xe2\x80\x99 Mount Kasios. Baal defeats Yam with two throwing clubs (thunderbolts?) named \xe2\x80\x98Expeller\xe2\x80\x99 and \xe2\x80\x98Chaser\xe2\x80\x99, which fly like eagles from the storm-god\xe2\x80\x99s hands. Other tablets associate the defeat of the snaky Yam with the slaying of a seven headed serpent <a href="/wiki/Lotan" title="Lotan">\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x99Ltn\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x99 (Litan/Lotan)</a>, apparently corresponding to the biblical <a href="/wiki/Leviathan" title="Leviathan">Leviathan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup></p>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Tarhunna_vs._Illuyanka">Tarhunna vs. Illuyanka</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Typhon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Tarhunna vs. Illuyanka">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Museum_of_Anatolian_Civilizations082_kopie1jpg.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="468" data-file-width="1279" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Museum_of_Anatolian_Civilizations082_kopie1jpg.jpg/300px-Museum_of_Anatolian_Civilizations082_kopie1jpg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Museum_of_Anatolian_Civilizations082_kopie1jpg.jpg/450px-Museum_of_Anatolian_Civilizations082_kopie1jpg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Museum_of_Anatolian_Civilizations082_kopie1jpg.jpg/600px-Museum_of_Anatolian_Civilizations082_kopie1jpg.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Museum_of_Anatolian_Civilizations082_kopie1jpg.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<a href="/wiki/Tar%E1%B8%ABunna" title="Tar\xe1\xb8\xabunna">Tarhunna</a> battles the serpent <a href="/wiki/Illuyanka" title="Illuyanka">Illuyanka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Anatolian_Civilizations" title="Museum of Anatolian Civilizations">Museum of Anatolian Civilizations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ankara" title="Ankara">Ankara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>From the north side of the Jebel Aqra, come <a href="/wiki/Hittites" title="Hittites">Hittite</a> myths, c. 1250 BC, which tell two versions of the storm-god <a href="/wiki/Tar%E1%B8%ABunna" title="Tar\xe1\xb8\xabunna">Tarhunna</a>\xe2\x80\x99s (Tarhunta\xe2\x80\x99s) battle against the serpent <a href="/wiki/Illuyanka" title="Illuyanka">Illuyanka(s)</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> In both of these versions, Tarhunna suffers an initial defeat against Illuyanka. In one version, Tarhunna seeks help from the goddess <a href="/wiki/Inara_(goddess)" title="Inara (goddess)">Inara</a>, who lures Illuyanka from his lair with a banquet, thereby enabling Tarhunna to surprise and kill Illuyanka. In the other version Illuyanka steels the heart and eyes of the defeated god, but Tarhunna\xe2\x80\x99s son marries a daughter of Illuyanka and is able to retrieve Tarhunna\xe2\x80\x99s stolen body parts, whereupon Tarhunna kills Illuyanka.</p>\n<p>These stories particularly resemble details found in the accounts of the Typhonomachy of Apollodorus, Oppian and Nonnus, which, though late accounts, possible preserve much earlier ones:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> The storm-god\xe2\x80\x99s initial defeat (Apollodorus, Nonnus), the loss of vital body parts (sinews: Apollodorus, Nonnus), the help of allies (Hermes and Aegipan: Apollodorus; Cadmos and Pan: Nonnus; Pan: Oppian), the luring of the serpentine opponent from his lair through the trickery of a banquet (Oppian, or by music: Nonnus).</p>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Teshub_vs_Hedammu_and_Ullikummi">Teshub vs Hedammu and Ullikummi</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Typhon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Teshub vs Hedammu and Ullikummi">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<p>Another c. 1250 BC Hittite text, derived from the <a href="/wiki/Hurrians" title="Hurrians">Hurrians</a>, tells of the Hurrian storm-god <a href="/wiki/Teshub" title="Teshub">Teshub</a> (with whom the Hittite\'s Tarhunna came to be identified) who lived on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mount_Hazzi" title="Mount Hazzi">Mount Hazzi</a>, the Hurrian name for the Jebel Aqra, and his battle with the sea-serpent Hedammu. Again the storm-god is aided by a goddess Sauska (equivalent to Inaru), who this time seduces the monster with music (as in Nonnus), drink, and sex, successfully luring the serpent from his lair in the sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> Just as the Typhonomachy can be seen as a sequel to the Titanomachy, a different Hittite text derived from the Hurrians, <i>The Song of Ullikummi</i>, a kind of sequel to the Hittite "kingship in heaven" succession myths of which the story of Teshub and Hedammu formed a part, tells of a second monster, this time made of stone, named Ullikummi that Teshub must defeat, in order to secure his rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup></p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Set">Set</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Typhon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Set">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>From apparently as early as <a href="/wiki/Hecataeus_of_Miletus" title="Hecataeus of Miletus">Hecataeus of Miletus</a> (c. 550 BC \xe2\x80\x93 c. 476 BC), Typhon was identified with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Set_(mythology)" title="Set (mythology)">Set</a>, the Egyptian god of destruction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> This syncretization with <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_mythology" title="Egyptian mythology">Egyptian mythology</a> can also be seen in the story, apparently known as early as Pindar, of Typhon chasing the gods to Egypt, and the gods transforming themselves into animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> Such a story arose perhaps as a way for the Greeks to explain Egypt\'s animal-shaped gods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> Herodotus also identified Typhon with Set, making him the second to last divine king of Egypt. Herodotus says that Typhon was deposed by <a href="/wiki/Osiris" title="Osiris">Osiris</a>\' son <a href="/wiki/Horus" title="Horus">Horus</a>, whom Herodutus equates with <a href="/wiki/Apollo" title="Apollo">Apollo</a> (with Osiris being equated with <a href="/wiki/Dionysus" title="Dionysus">Dionysus</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> and after his defeat by Horus, Typhon was "supposed to have been hidden" in the "<a href="/wiki/Serbonian_Bog" title="Serbonian Bog">Serbonian marsh</a>" (identified with modern <a href="/wiki/Lake_Bardawil" title="Lake Bardawil">Lake Bardawil</a>) in Egypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Confused_with_the_Giants">Confused with the Giants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Typhon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Confused with the Giants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Typhon bears a close resemblance to an older generation of descendants of Gaia, the <a href="/wiki/Giants_(Greek_mythology)" title="Giants (Greek mythology)">Giants</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> They, like their younger brother Typhon after them, challenged Zeus for supremacy of the cosmos,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> were (in later representations) shown as snake-footed,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> and end up buried under volcanos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup></p>\n<p>While distinct in early accounts, in later accounts Typhon was often considered to be one of the <a href="/wiki/Giants_(Greek_mythology)" title="Giants (Greek mythology)">Giants</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> The Roman mythographer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyginus" title="Hyginus">Hyginus</a> (64 BC \xe2\x80\x93 17 AD) includes Typhon in his list of Giants,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> while the Roman poet <a href="/wiki/Horace" title="Horace">Horace</a> (65 \xe2\x80\x93 8 BC), mentions Typhon, along with the Giants <a href="/wiki/Mimas_(Giant)" title="Mimas (Giant)">Mimas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Porphyrion" title="Porphyrion">Porphyrion</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Enceladus_(mythology)" title="Enceladus (mythology)">Enceladus</a>, as together battling <a href="/wiki/Athena" title="Athena">Athena</a>, during the Gigantomachy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Astronomica_(Manilius)" title="Astronomica (Manilius)">Astronomica</a></i>, attributed to the 1st-century AD Roman poet and astrologer <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Manilius" title="Marcus Manilius">Marcus Manilius</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> and the late 4th-century early 5th-century Greek poet <a href="/wiki/Nonnus" title="Nonnus">Nonnus</a>, also consider Typhon to be one of the Giants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Typhon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: href="http://www.iconiclimc.ch/visitors/treesearch.php?source=100&amp;term=%22Typhon+14%22" rel="nofollow">Typhon 14</a>.</span></li>\n<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyginus" title="Hyginus">Hyginus</a>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fabulae" title="Fabulae">Fabulae</a></i> <a class="external text" However Hyginus, <i>Fabulae</i> <a class="external text" href="http://www.theoi.com/Text/HyginusFabulae4.html" rel="nofollow">152</a> has Typhon as the offspring of Tartarus and Tartara, where, according to Fontenrose, <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=wqeVv09Y6hIC&amp;pg=PA77" rel="nofollow">p. 77</a>, Tartara was "no doubt [Gaia] herself under a name which designates all that lies beneath the earth."</span></li>\n<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Bibliotheca_(Pseudo-Apollodorus)" title="Bibliotheca (Pseudo-Apollodorus)">Apollodorus</a>, <a class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Apollod.+1.6.3" rel="nofollow">1.6.3</a>.</span></li>\n<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Aeschylus" title="Aeschylus">Aeschylus</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Seven_Against_Thebes" title="Seven Against Thebes">Seven Against Thebes</a></i> <a class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A1999.01.0014%3Acard%3D521" rel="nofollow">522\xe2\x80\x93523</a>; Orthrus as his brother.</span></li>\n<li id="cite_note-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-37">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Acusilaus" id="cite_note-39"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-39">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Hesiod" title="Hesiod">Hesiod</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Theogony"
