The first to name the dragon Ladon is <a href="/wiki/Apollonius_of_Rhodes" title="Apollonius of Rhodes">Apollonius of Rhodes</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Argonautica" title="Argonautica">Argonautica</a></i> <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/stream/argonautica00apoluoft#page/388/mode/2up" rel="nofollow">4.1396 (pp. 388\xe2\x80\x93389)</a>, which makes Ladon earthborn, see Fowler 2013, <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=scd8AQAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PA28" rel="nofollow">p. 28 n. 97</a>. <a href="/wiki/John_Tzetzes" title="John Tzetzes">Tzetzes</a>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chiliades" title="Chiliades">Chiliades</a></i> 2.36.360 (Kiessling, <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=dG0GAAAAQAAJ&amp;pg=PA54" rel="nofollow">p. 54</a>; English translation: Berkowitz, <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/stream/TzetzesCHILIADES/Chiliades#page/n31/mode/1up" rel="nofollow">p. 33</a>), also has Typhon as Ladon\'s father.</span></li>\n<li id="cite_note-43"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-43">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Lasus_of_Hermione" title="Lasus of Hermione">Lasus of Hermione</a>, fragment 706A (Campbell, pp. 310\xe2\x80\x93311). <a href="/wiki/Euripides" title="Euripides">Euripides</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Phoenician_Women" title="The Phoenician Women">The Phoenician Women</a></i> <a class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A1999.01.0118%3Acard%3D1019" rel="nofollow">1019\xe2\x80\x931020</a>; Ogden 2013a, <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=FQ2pAK9luwkC&amp;pg=PA149" rel="nofollow">p. 149 n. 3</a> has Echidna as her mother, without mentioning a father. Hesiod mentions the <a href="/wiki/Sphinx" title="Sphinx">Sphinx</a> (and the <a href="/wiki/Nemean_lion" title="Nemean lion">Nemean lion</a>) as having been the offspring of Echidna\'s son Orthrus, by another ambiguous "she", in line 326 (see Clay, <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=2etBN0w0NGUC&amp;pg=PA159" rel="nofollow">p.159, with n. 34</a>), read variously as the Chimera, Echidna herself, or even Ceto.</span></li>\n<li id="cite_note-44"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-44">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Bibliotheca_(Pseudo-Apollodorus)" title="Bibliotheca (Pseudo-Apollodorus)">Apollodorus</a>, <i>Library</i> <a class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Apollod.+2.5.10" rel="nofollow">2.5.10</a> (Orthrus), <a class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Apollod.+2.3.1" rel="nofollow">2.3.1</a> (Chimera), <a class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Apollod.+2.5.11" rel="nofollow">2.5.11</a> (Caucasian Eagle), <a class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Apollod.+2.5.11" rel="nofollow">2.5.11</a> (Ladon), <a class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Apollod.+3.5.8" rel="nofollow">3.5.8</a> (Sphinx), <a class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Apollod.+2.5.1" rel="nofollow">2.5.1</a> (Nemean lion), <i>Epitome</i> <a class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus:text:1999.01.0022:text=Epitome:book=E:chapter=1" rel="nofollow">1.1</a> (Crommyonian Sow).</span></li>\n<li id="cite_note-45"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-45">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyginus" title="Hyginus">Hyginus</a>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fabulae" title="Fabulae">Fabulae</a></i> <a class="external text" href="http://www.theoi.com/Text/HyginusFabulae1.html" rel="nofollow">Preface</a>, <a class="external text" href="http://www.theoi.com/Text/HyginusFabulae4.html" rel="nofollow">151</a>.</span></li>\n<li id="cite_note-46"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-46">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Compare with <a href="/wiki/Apollonius_of_Rhodes" title="Apollonius of Rhodes">Apollonius of Rhodes</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Argonautica" title="Argonautica">Argonautica</a></i>, <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/stream/argonautica00apoluoft#page/184/mode/2up" rel="nofollow">2.1208\xe2\x80\x931215 (pp. 184\xe2\x80\x93185)</a>, where the dragon is the offspring of <a href="/wiki/Gaia_(mythology)" title="Gaia (mythology)">Gaia</a> by Typhon (Ho\xc5\xa1ek, p. 678).</span></li>\n<li id="cite_note-47"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-47">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See also <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a>, <i>Ciris</i> 67; Lyne, <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=WMf_VULZvLUC&amp;pg=130" rel="nofollow">pp. 130\xe2\x80\x93131</a>. Others give other parents for Scylla. Several authors name <a href="/wiki/Crataeis" title="Crataeis">Crataeis</a> as the mother of Scylla, see <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Odyssey" title="Odyssey">Odyssey</a></i> <a class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text;jsessionid=0C3862DF72BDE338E6D62A24A49FEF27?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A1999.01.0136%3Abook%3D12%3Acard%3D111" rel="nofollow">12.124\xe2\x80\x93125</a>; <a href="/wiki/Ovid" title="Ovid">Ovid</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Metamorphoses" title="Metamorphoses">Metamorphoses</a></i> <a class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A1999.02.0028%3Abook%3D13%3Acard%3D705" rel="nofollow">13.749</a>; <a href="/wiki/Bibliotheca_(Pseudo-Apollodorus)" title="Bibliotheca (Pseudo-Apollodorus)">Apollodorus</a>, <a class="external text" href="http://data.perseus.org/citations/urn:cts:greekLit:tlg0548.tlg002.perseus-eng1:e.7.20" rel="nofollow">E7.20</a>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Servius" title="Servius">Servius</a> on <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Aeneid" title="Aeneid">Aeneid</a></i> 3.420; and schol. on <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>, <a href="/wiki/Republic" title="Republic">Republic</a> 9.588c. Neither Homer nor Ovid mention a father, but Apollodorus says that the father was Trienus (or <a href="/wiki/Triton_(mythology)" title="Triton (mythology)">Triton</a>?) or Phorcus, similarly the Plato scholiast, perhaps following Apollodorus, gives the father as Tyrrhenus or Phorcus, while <a href="/wiki/Eustathius_of_Thessalonica" title="Eustathius of Thessalonica">Eustathius</a> on Homer, <i>Odyssey</i> 12.85 gives the father as Triton. The Hesiodic <i><a href="/wiki/Megalai_Ehoiai" title="Megalai Ehoiai">Megalai Ehoiai</a></i> (fr. 262 MW = Most <a class="external text" href="http://www.loebclassics.com/view/hesiod-other_fragments/2007/pb_LCL503.277.xml" rel="nofollow">200</a>) gives Hecate and <a href="/wiki/Phorbas" title="Phorbas">Phorbas</a> as the parents of Scylla, while <a href="/wiki/Acusilaus" title="Acusilaus">Acusilaus</a>, fr. 42 Fowler (Fowler 2013, <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=scd8AQAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PA32" rel="nofollow">p. 32</a>) says that Scylla\'s parents were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hekate" title="Hekate">Hekate</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phorkys" title="Phorkys">Phorkys</a> (so also schol. <i><a href="/wiki/Odyssey" title="Odyssey">Odyssey</a></i> 12.85). <a href="/wiki/Apollonius_of_Rhodes" title="Apollonius of Rhodes">Apollonius of Rhodes</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Argonautica" title="Argonautica">Argonautica</a></i> <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/stream/argonautica00apoluoft#page/350/mode/2up" rel="nofollow">4. 828\xe2\x80\x93829 (pp. 350\xe2\x80\x93351)</a> says that "Hecate who is called Crataeis," and Phorcys were the parents of Scylla. Semos of Delos (<i>FGrHist</i> 396 F 22) says that Crataeis was the daughter of Hekate and Triton, and mother of Scylla by Deimos. <a href="/wiki/Stesichorus" title="Stesichorus">Stesichorus</a>, <a class="external text" href="http://www.loebclassics.com/view/stesichorus_i-fragments/1991/pb_LCL476.133.xml?result=1&amp;rskey=vkJkZt" rel="nofollow">F220 <i>PMG</i> (Campbell, pp. 132\xe2\x80\x93133)</a> has Lamia as the mother of Scylla, possibly the <a href="/wiki/Lamia_(daughter_of_Poseidon)" title="Lamia (daughter of Poseidon)">Lamia</a> who was the daughter of <a href="/wiki/Poseidon" title="Poseidon">Poseidon</a>. For discussions of the parentage of Scylla, see Fowler 2013, <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=scd8AQAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PA32" rel="nofollow">p. 32</a>, Ogden 2013a, <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=FQ2pAK9luwkC&amp;pg=PA134" rel="nofollow">p. 134</a>; Gantz, pp. 731\xe2\x80\x93732; and Frazer\'s note to Apollodorus, <a class="external text" href="http://data.perseus.org/citations/urn:cts:greekLit:tlg0548.tlg002.perseus-eng1:e.7.20" rel="nofollow">E7.20</a>.</span></li>\n<li id="cite_note-48"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-48">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Hesiod, <i><a href="/wiki/Theogony" title="Theogony">Theogony</a></i>, <a class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A1999.01.0130%3Acard%3D240" rel="nofollow">265\xe2\x80\x93269</a>; so also <a href="/wiki/Bibliotheca_(Pseudo-Apollodorus)" title="Bibliotheca (Pseudo-Apollodorus)">Apollodorus</a>, <a class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Apollod.+1.2.6" rel="nofollow">1.2.6</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyginus" title="Hyginus">Hyginus</a>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fabulae" title="Fabulae">Fabulae</a></i> <a class="external text" href="http://www.theoi.com/Text/HyginusFabulae1.html" rel="nofollow">Preface</a> (though <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fabulae" title="Fabulae">Fabulae</a></i> <a class="external text" href="http://www.theoi.com/Text/HyginusFabulae1.html#14" rel="nofollow">14</a>, gives their parents as Thaumas and Oxomene). In the <a href="/wiki/Epimenides" title="Epimenides">Epimenides</a> <i>Theogony</i> (3B7) they are the daughters of <a href="/wiki/Oceanus" title="Oceanus">Oceanus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gaia_(mythology)" title="Gaia (mythology)">Gaia</a>, while in <a href="/wiki/Pherecydes_of_Syros" title="Pherecydes of Syros">Pherecydes of Syros</a> (7B5) they are the daughters of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boreas_(god)" title="Boreas (god)">Boreas</a> (Gantz, p. 18).</span></li>\n<li id="cite_note-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-49">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Gaius_Valerius_Flaccus" title="Gaius Valerius Flaccus">Valerius Flaccus</a>, <i>Argonautica</i> <a class="external text" href="http://www.theoi.com/Text/ValeriusFlaccus4.html" rel="nofollow">4.428, 516</a>.</span></li>\n<li id="cite_note-50"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-50">^</a></b></span> According to West 1966, p. 251, "This identification [Arimoi as Aramaeans] has been repeated in modern times." For example for Fontenrose, <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=wqeVv09Y6hIC&amp;pg=PA71" rel="nofollow">p. 71</a>, the "Arimoi, it seems fairly certain, are the Aramaeans, and the country is either Syria or Cilicia, most likely the latter, since in later sources that is usually Typhon\'s land." But see Fox Lane, pp. <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=NZFM-TUwFxgC&amp;pg=PA107" rel="nofollow">107</a>, <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=NZFM-TUwFxgC&amp;pg=PA291" rel="nofollow">291\xe2\x80\x93298</a>, which rejects this identification, instead arguing for the derivation of "Arima" from the <a href="/wiki/Hittites" title="Hittites">Hittite</a> place names "Erimma" and "Arimmatta".</span></li>\n<li id="cite_note-117"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-117">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a>, <a class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Strab.+12.8.19" rel="nofollow">12.8.19</a>.</span></li>\n<li id="cite_note-118"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-118">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a>, <a class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A1999.01.0198%3Abook%3D13%3Achapter%3D4%3Asection%3D6" rel="nofollow">13.4.11</a>.</span></li>\n<li id="cite_note-119"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-119">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a>, <a class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A1999.01.0198%3Abook%3D13%3Achapter%3D4%3Asection%3D6" rel="nofollow">13.4.6</a>. West 1966, p. 381; Lane Fox, 298, <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=NZFM-TUwFxgC&amp;pg=PA405" rel="nofollow">p. 405</a>, <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=NZFM-TUwFxgC&amp;pg=PA407" rel="nofollow">p. 407 n. 53</a>; Ogden 2013a, <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=FQ2pAK9luwkC&amp;pg=PA152" rel="nofollow">pp. 152\xe2\x80\x93153</a>. Fontenrose: "His name is very likely not Greek; but though non-Hellenic etymologies have been suggested, I doubt that its meaning is now recoverable"; West: "The origin of the name and its variant forms is unexplained"; Lane Fox: "a name of uncertain derivation". Given that Typhoeus and Typhaon are apparently the earlier forms, Ogden, suggests that theories based upon the assumption that "Typhon" is the primary form "seem ill-founded", and says that it "seems much more likely" that the form Typhon arose from "the desire to assimilate this <i>drak\xc5\x8dn\'</i>s name to the name shape of other <i>drakontes,</i> perhaps Python in particular".</span></li>\n<li id="cite_note-126"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-126">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">West 1966, pp. 252, 381; Ogden 2013a, <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=FQ2pAK9luwkC&amp;pg=PA152" rel="nofollow">p. 152</a>, both citing Worms 1953. But according to West "it is far from certain that there is any real etymological connection ... [Typhon\'s] association with the tornado is secondary, and due to popular etymology. It may have already influenced Hesiod, for there is at present no better explanation of the fact that irregular stormwinds (especially those met at sea) are made the children of [Typhon]." For the use of the words <i>\xcf\x84\xe1\xbf\xa1\xcf\x86\xcf\x8e\xcf\x82, \xcf\x84\xe1\xbf\xa1\xcf\x86\xe1\xbf\xb6\xce\xbd</i> meaning "whirlwind" see <i>LSJ</i>, <a class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A1999.04.0057%3Aentry%3D*tufw%2Fs" rel="nofollow">\xce\xa4\xcf\x85_\xcf\x86\xcf\x8e\xcf\x82 , \xe1\xbf\xb6, \xe1\xbd\x81</a>; <i><a href="/wiki/Suda" title="Suda">Suda</a></i> s.v. <a class="external text" href="http://www.stoa.org/sol-bin//search.pl?login=&amp;enlogin=&amp;db=REAL&amp;field=adlerhw_gr&amp;searchstr=tau,422" rel="nofollow">Tetuph\xc3\xb4mai</a>, <a class="external text" href="http://www.stoa.org/sol-bin//search.pl?login=&amp;enlogin=&amp;db=REAL&amp;field=adlerhw_gr&amp;searchstr=tau,1224" rel="nofollow">Tuph\xc3\xb4n</a>, <a class="external text" href="http://www.stoa.org/sol-bin//search.pl?login=&amp;enlogin=&amp;db=REAL&amp;field=adlerhw_gr&amp;searchstr=tau,1228" rel="nofollow">Tuph\xc3\xb4s</a>; Lane Fox, p. 298, which offers the derivation of Typhon from the Greek <i>t\xc5\xabph\xc5\x8dn</i> ("smoking", "smouldering"), though Ogden 2013a, <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=FQ2pAK9luwkC&amp;pg=PA153" rel="nofollow">p. 153</a>, while conceding that such a derivation "fits Typhon\'s nature and condition in life and death so perfectly", objects (n. 23) "why would the participle-style declension in <i>\xe2\x80\x93\xcf\x89\xce\xbd, \xe2\x80\x93\xce\xbf\xce\xbd\xcf\x84\xce\xbf\xcf\x82</i> have been substituted with one in <i>\xe2\x80\x93\xe1\xbf\xb6\xce\xbd, \xe2\x80\x93\xe1\xbf\xb6\xce\xbd\xce\xbf\xcf\x82</i>?"</span></li>\n<li id="cite_note-128"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-128">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Watkins, <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=JrvnCwAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PA460" rel="nofollow">pp. 460\xe2\x80\x93463</a>; Although Gaia\'s attitude toward Zeus in Hesiod\'s <i>Theogony</i> is mostly benevolent: protecting him as a child (479 ff.), helping him to defeat the Titans (626\xe2\x80\x93628), advising him to swallow Metis, thereby protecting him from overthrow, (890\xe2\x80\x93894). she also (apparently with malice) gives birth to Zeus\' worst enemy Typhon; West 1966, p. 24, sees in the Tiamat story a possible explanation for this "odd little inconsistency".</span></li>\n<li id="cite_note-150"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-150">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Fontenrose, <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=wqeVv09Y6hIC&amp;pg=PA145" rel="nofollow">p. 145</a>; Watkins, pp. 448 ff.; Ogden 2013a, pp. <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=FQ2pAK9luwkC&amp;pg=PA14" rel="nofollow">14\xe2\x80\x9315</a>, <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=FQ2pAK9luwkC&amp;pg=PA75" rel="nofollow">75</a>.
